reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a transposed convolution, applies Mish activation, adds a value, 
    applies Hardtanh activation, and scales the output.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, add_value, scale):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding)
        self.add_value = add_value
        self.scale = scale

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = torch.nn.functional.mish(x) # Mish activation
        x = x + self.add_value
        x = torch.nn.functional.hardtanh(x, min_val=-1, max_val=1) # Hardtanh activation
        x = x * self.scale # Scaling
        return x

batch_size = 128
in_channels  = 64  
out_channels = 64  
height = width = 128  
kernel_size  = 3
stride       = 2  
padding      = 1
output_padding = 1
add_value = 0.5
scale = 2

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, add_value, scale]

However, the main difference is that in the reference, out0 is written after conv, then mish is computed and written as out1. Then add is computed and written as out2, etc. In our fused kernel, we compute the entire chain in registers and write once. This reduces rounding error (more accurate). The reference has rounding after each step. So our output will differ slightly (usually by less than the separate rounding). But maybe we can implement a specialized kernel for this specific case: in_channels=64, out_channels=64, kernel=3, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1. This is like a 2x upsampling convolution. But it's not exactly sub-pixel. We could think of it as: The input is 128x128. The output is 256x256. Since stride=2, each output pixel is influenced by at most ceil(kernel/stride)^2 = 4 input pixels? Actually, for transposed conv, each output pixel is a weighted sum of a neighborhood in the input. The receptive field is smaller. Let's think in terms of standard conv: If we have a standard conv with stride=2, kernel=3, padding=1, the mapping from input to output is many-to-one. The transposed conv is the adjoint: one output pixel maps from multiple input pixels. The formula for transposed conv: out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh,kw} input[n, c_in, (h+padding-kh)/stride, (w+padding-kw)/stride] * weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] when the indices are integer and in range. Wait, the exact formula for transposed convolution (as implemented in PyTorch) is: For each n, c_out, h, w: out[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh=0}^{KH-1} sum_{kw=0}^{KW-1} input[n, c_in, (h + padding - kh) / stride, (w + padding - kw) / stride] * weight[c_in, c_out, KH - 1 - kh, KW - 1 - kw] where division is integer and only when (h + padding - kh) % stride == 0 and similarly for w. Alternatively, there is an effective kernel flip. But the key is: each output pixel depends on at most ceil(KH/stride) * ceil(KW/stride) = ceil(3/2)=2 => 2*2=4 input pixels. Actually, for each (kh, kw), there is at most one (h_in, w_in) that maps to a given (h_out, w_out). Since we iterate over kh and kw (9 values), we check divisibility. But for a given (h_out, w_out), the condition (h_out + padding - kh) % stride == 0 means kh ≡ h_out + padding (mod stride). Since kh ranges from 0 to 2, there are at most ceil(3/2)=2 values of kh that satisfy the congruence? Wait, stride=2. For a given h_out, h_out + padding - kh must be even. Since padding=1, h_out + 1 - kh must be even => kh ≡ h_out + 1 (mod 2). So exactly half of the kh values satisfy it: if K=3, either 1 or 2 values of kh. For each valid kh, there is exactly one h_in. So there are at most 2 * 2 = 4 contributions. Actually, for a given h_out, kh can be 0,1,2. Exactly 1 or 2 of them will have the right parity. Vectorized kernel: ```c++ __global__ void fused_kernel_vec(float* __restrict__ data, float add_val, float scale_val, int64_t n) { int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; // Process 4 elements at a time int64_t n_vec = n / 4; float4* data4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(data); for (int64_t i = idx; i < n_vec; i += stride) { float4 x4 = data4[i]; // process each component float x0 = x4.x; // ... compute ... x4.x = out0; x4.y = out1; x4.z = out2; x4.w = out3; data4[i] = x4; } // Tail for (int64_t i = idx + n_vec * 4; i < n; i += stride) { // scalar } } ``` But using `float4` requires alignment. The compiler couldn't find `cuda_runtime.h` because we only passed `cpp_sources` and not `cuda_sources`. `load_inline` has separate `cpp_sources` (compiled with host C++ compiler) and `cuda_sources` (compiled with nvcc). CUDA headers can only be included in `cuda_sources`. Let me put all the code in `cuda_sources` instead.

reasoning
The macro definitions are disabling some half/bfloat16 support, but the actual error is that `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` doesn't compile because `ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h` might not be automatically included. Let me just use `c10::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()`. That's the standard PyTorch way and works in extension code.

turn 4llm latency: 15.1s
reasoning
The issue is that `at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` and `c10::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream()` are not available when the CUDA source is compiled with PyTorch's `load_inline` because the header `ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h` might not be included. We can simply include the right header or avoid using stream altogether (default stream is fine). Or we can build with `cudaStream_t stream = 0;` (default stream). Let me try using the default stream (0) or including the right header.

Looking at the reference more carefully: ```python self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1) ``` With kernel_size=3, stride=2. A common optimization trick: transposed convolutions with stride=2 can be implemented using "sub-pixel convolution" or by reshaping. Specifically, if we have a stride-2 transposed convolution with odd kernel size, it's equivalent to: 1. Upsample by factor 2 using `F.interpolate` with `mode='nearest'` or outputting zeros between pixels 2. Apply a stride-1 convolution with appropriate padding But for exact mathematical equivalence with ConvTranspose2d, we need zero-insertion upsampling, not nearest-neighbor interpolation. Zero-insertion can be done with `F.conv_transpose2d` or by reshaping: `x.view(B, C, H, 1, W, 1)` and then padding with zeros. In PyTorch: ```python x_upsampled = torch.zeros(B, C, H*2, W*2, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) x_upsampled[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x out = F.conv2d(x_upsampled, self.conv_transpose.weight, padding=1) ``` Wait, is the weight the same? Let's think about the relationship. A transposed convolution with stride=2, kernel=3, padding=1, output_padding=1 on input of size 128x128 gives output 256x256. If we upsample by inserting zeros: input 128x128 -> 256x256 (with zeros). Then apply a stride-1 conv with kernel=3, padding=1, which gives 256x256. This is mathematically equivalent! And the weights need to be the same (transposed conv weight vs conv weight). In PyTorch, `ConvTranspose2d.weight` has shape `[in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, kernel_size]` for the transposed conv. Wait, no. In PyTorch's `ConvTranspose2d`, the weight shape is `[in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, kernel_size]`, but when computing output, it's used as `[out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, kernel_size]`. Actually, looking at the docs: > weight (Tensor) – the learnable weights of the module of shape (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1]) But during forward, the weight is internally transposed. For the equivalent `Conv2d` after zero-insertion, the weight shape should be `[out_channels, in_channels, kernel_size, kernel_size]`, and the values should be the transposed weight. Actually, let me verify: in a regular `Conv2d(in, out, k, s, p)`, the weight is `[out, in, k, k]`. In `ConvTranspose2d(in, out, k, s, p, op)`, the weight is `[in, out, k, k]`. If we do zero-insertion + Conv2d, we'd need weights of shape `[out, in, k, k]`, which is the transpose of `ConvTranspose2d`'s weight. So yes: ```python # Equivalent to conv_transpose(x) where x is [B, C_in, H, W] x_up = torch.zeros(B, C_in, H*2, W*2, device=x.device) x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x out = F.conv2d(x_up, self.conv_transpose.weight.transpose(0, 1), padding=1) ``` Wait, `ConvTranspose2d`'s `.weight` is `[in_channels, out_channels, k, k]`. Actually, the transposed convolution operation uses the same kernel spatially but computes a different operation. Let me think more carefully. For the zero-insertion + stride-1 conv equivalence: - Upsample input by stride (insert zeros). - Apply standard conv with stride=1 and padding=k-1-p. Wait, I need to check the exact mapping. Apply a standard convolution with the same weight (but transposed/rotated) Let me look up the exact formula. Actually, there's a known equivalence: ConvTranspose2d(in, out, k, s, p, op) applied to x is equivalent to: 1. Insert (s-1) zeros between each element in both H and W dimensions. 2. Pad the result with (k-p-1) zeros on each side. 3. Apply Conv2d(in, out, k, stride=1, padding=0) with weight = the original weight but spatially flipped (rotated 180°) and channels transposed. So the weight needs to be rotated 180 degrees and transposed in the channel dimension. In our case, since we can't change the weight (it's initialized randomly from nn.ConvTranspose2d), but we do have access to it in `__init__` via `self.conv_transpose.weight`. We could compute the equivalent `Conv2d` weight in `__init__`: ```python # ConvTranspose2d weight shape: [in_channels, out_channels, kH, kW] # Equivalent Conv2d weight: flip spatial dims, transpose channels # Shape: [out_channels, in_channels, kH, kW] weight_t = self.conv_transpose.weight.transpose(0, 1).flip(dims=[2, 3]) ``` Then in forward: ```python B, C, H, W = x.shape x_up = torch.zeros(B, C, H*2, W*2, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x out = F.conv2d(x_up, weight_t, padding=1) ``` Wait, does the padding need to be 1? Let me verify with the formula. After zero insertion, we have size (2H, 2W). We pad with (k-p-1) = (3-1-1) = 1 on each side. Because padding=1 on (2H, 2W) gives the same effect as padding 0 on (2H+2, 2W+2). For the weight: Conv2d needs the weight to be [out_channels, in_channels, k, k]. Our ConvTranspose2d weight is [in, out, k, k]. And we need it spatially flipped. Let me verify. In transposed convolution, the operation can be viewed as the gradient of a regular convolution. The gradient w.r.t. input of conv2d(input, weight) is conv_transpose2d(grad_output, weight). There's a known relationship. If T is the transposed convolution operation using weight W_t (shape [in, out, k, k]), and C is the regular convolution with zero insertion using weight W_c (shape [out, in, k, k]), then for them to be equivalent: W_c = W_t.transpose(0, 1).flip(2, 3). Let me verify with a simple example using PyTorch. Actually, I should test this to be sure. But since I can't execute direct test code easily, let me think more carefully. In 1D: ConvTranspose1d with weight w = [w0, w1, w2] (length 3), stride=2. Output at position i is a dot product of w with selected input values. Actually, let's think of transposed conv as convolving the input with a kernel that is "dilated" by inserting stride-1 zeros between weights. No wait, that's atrous convolution. Another way: Transposed convolution with stride=2 is equivalent to: 1. For each input, multiply it by the kernel weight and scatter into output at stride positions. 2. Sum all contributions. So for input x[i], its contribution to output y is: y[i*2 + j] += x[i] * W[j] for j = 0, 1, 2 (with appropriate boundary handling and padding). For zero-upsampled input x_up where x_up[i*2] = x[i], x_up[i*2+1] = 0: Applying a stride-1 conv with kernel w' and padding=1: y[i] = sum_{j=0}^2 x_up[i + j - 1] * w'[j]. Actually, in ConvTranspose with padding=1, output at position p gets contributions from input positions i such that (p + padding - j) / stride = i for some j. That is, i = floor((p + 1 - j)/2). Let's enumerate for output positions p: For p = 2k: i could be k if j=1 (since (2k+1-1)/2 = k), or k-1 if j=3? No j only 0,1,2. (2k+1-0)/2 = k + 0.5 → not integer. (2k+1-1)/2 = k → integer, j=1. (2k+1-2)/2 = k - 0.5 → not integer. So y[2k] = x[k] * W[1]. For p = 2k+1: (2k+1+1-0)/2 = k+1 → integer, j=0. (2k+1+1-1)/2 = k + 0.5 → not integer. (2k+1+1-2)/2 = k → integer, j=2. So y[2k+1] = x[k+1] * W[0] + x[k] * W[2]. Comparing with conv2d on upsampled input: y[2k] = x[k] * w'[1] y[2k+1] = x[k] * w'[0] + x[k+1] * w'[2] So we need: w'[1] = W[1], w'[0] = W[2], w'[2] = W[0]. That is, w'[j] = W[2-j]. The kernel needs to be reversed (flipped)! For 2D: W'[j0, j1] = W[2-j0, 2-j1], which is a 180-degree rotation (flip both dims). And for the channel dimension: in conv2d, output channel c_out is computed as sum over c_in of input[c_in] * W'[c_out, c_in]. In conv_transpose2d, output channel c_out gets contributions from all input channels c_in, and the weight is W_t[c_in, c_out]. So W' should have shape [out, in, ...] with W'[c_out, c_in] = W_t[c_in, c_out, ...] flipped spatially. So: `W' = W_t.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(dims=[2, 3])`. Given this, the replacement is: ```python self.conv = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=1, bias=False) self.conv.weight = nn.Parameter(self.conv_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(dims=[2, 3])) ``` And in forward: ```python B, C, H, W = x.shape x_up = torch.zeros(B, C, H*self.stride, W*self.stride, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) x_up[:, :, ::self.stride, ::self.stride] = x out = self.conv(x_up) ``` Wait, stride is 2. Now, will this be faster? - Creating `x_up` with `torch.zeros` requires allocating memory and initializing it. But we could use `torch.zeros` which is fast. - `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x` is a scattered write, which might be slow due to uncoalesced memory writes. - However, `conv2d` with stride=1 on 256x256 might be significantly faster than `conv_transpose2d` with stride=2 on 128x128, because cuDNN is more optimized for regular convolutions than transposed convolutions. Actually, on modern GPUs and cuDNN, transposed convolutions are often implemented using winograd or implicit GEMM algorithms similar to regular conv. But sometimes regular conv is better optimized. Since the input to regular conv is 256x256 (4x larger spatially) but with 75% zeros, cuDNN might be smart enough to handle it. But zero-insertion means many wasted multiply-adds. The regular conv on 256x256 with 64 channels requires MACs = 64*64*3*3*256*256 = ~2.4 billion. The transposed conv on 128x128 also requires roughly the same number of MACs (since it's the gradient). So computationally they're equivalent. But the memory bandwidth for regular conv might be better because: - Transposed conv often involves strided memory accesses or data expansion that is less cache-friendly. - Regular conv on a contiguous 256x256 tensor might have better memory locality. However, the scattered write `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x` is problematic. On a 256x256 tensor, writing to every other element in both dimensions means each warp writes to non-contiguous memory, resulting in poor coalescing. Each thread writes a float, but adjacent threads write to locations separated by 2*256*4 = 2048 bytes. So each warp of 32 threads would need 32 cache lines (or sectors), when ideally it would need 2. This is terrible write coalescing. Actually, we can use a different approach to avoid the scattered write: `F.interpolate(x, scale_factor=2, mode='nearest')` gives a tensor that is contiguous and doesn't have scattered writes. But it's not mathematically equivalent because it duplicates values instead of inserting zeros. We could also use `torch.nn.functional.max_unpool2d` with a pool of size 1? No, max_unpool requires indices. Another approach: `x.repeat_interleave(2, dim=2).repeat_interleave(2, dim=3)` duplicates values, not zeros. How to efficiently create the upsampled tensor with zeros? We could use `torch.zeros` and then `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x`. The write is indeed uncoalesced. The thread iterates over all input positions and kernel weights that influence that output pixel. The trade-off is: - cuDNN with bias=True: conv computes output + bias in one go, writes to memory once. - cuDNN with bias=False: conv writes output to memory. One more thing: I should make sure my kernel handles large tensors correctly. With `n` as `int64_t`, and `blocks` as `int`, if `blocks` exceeds `INT_MAX`, there could be problems. We can cap it at, say, 1024 blocks and let the grid-stride loop handle the rest. This is a common optimization in PyTorch's elementwise kernels. Actually, in my original kernel, I didn't use a grid-stride loop correctly. Let me check: ```c int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; for (int64_t i = idx; i < n; i += stride) { ``` Yes, this is a grid-stride loop. With 1,048,576 blocks of 512 threads, stride = 536,870,912 = n. So each thread processes either 1 element (if idx < n) or 0 elements. The grid-stride loop adds no overhead because `idx < n` terminates immediately for threads beyond n. Actually, launching 1,048,576 blocks might have some overhead but shouldn't be too bad. But many CUDA experts recommend using fewer blocks (e.g., 256 * number_of_SMs) for elementwise ops to amortize launch overhead. For H100 with 132 SMs, using e.g., 1024 blocks gives good occupancy. I'll cap blocks at 1024. Actually, a more principled approach is: ```c int blocks = min((int)((n + threads - 1) / threads), 1024); ``` Or follow PyTorch's heuristic: `max_blocks = 8192` or `max_blocks = 256 * num_SMs`. Also, I should double-check my mish implementation against PyTorch more carefully. PyTorch's mish: ```python def mish(input, inplace=False): return input * torch.tanh(F.softplus(input)) ``` PyTorch's softplus for float: ```python def softplus(x): return torch.log(1 + torch.exp(x)) # with stability ``` The actual PyTorch implementation uses: ```cpp // For x > threshold: return x // For x <= threshold: return std::log1p(std::exp(x)) float softplus(float x) { if (x > 20) return x; return log1p(exp(x)); } ``` Wait, is that PyTorch's actual formula? Actually, PyTorch's softplus formula is: ```python softplus(x) = log(1 + exp(x)) ``` with numerical stability for large negative values, where `exp(x)` underflows to 0 so `log(1+0)=0`. For large positive values, it just returns `x` to avoid overflow in `exp(x)`. The threshold where `exp(x)` would overflow float32 is around 88. But PyTorch uses a lower threshold for performance, commonly around 20. My implementation: `sp = fmaxf(x, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(x)))` This is always exact (to within exp/log precision) and: - For large positive x: `expf(-x)` underflows to 0, `log1pf(0) = 0`, `sp = x + 0 = x`. - For large negative x: `expf(x)` underflows to 0, `log1pf(0) = 0`, `sp = 0 + 0 = 0`. - For x = 20: `expf(-20) = 2.06e-9`, `log1pf(2.06e-9) ≈ 2.06e-9`, `sp = 20 + 2.06e-9 ≈ 20`. - For x = -20: `expf(-20) = 2.06e-9`, `sp = 0 + 2.06e-9 ≈ 2.06e-9`. PyTorch's softplus at x=20: `log1p(exp(20)) = log1p(4.85e8) ≈ log(4.85e8) ≈ 20.0`. So effectively x. PyTorch's softplus at x=88: `exp(88)` overflows to inf, `log1p(inf)` = inf. Wait, PyTorch uses a threshold to avoid this. For float32, exp(88) ≈ 1.65e38 which is near float max. PyTorch's threshold for softplus is typically 20. So for x > 20, PyTorch returns x. My formula returns x + tiny_value which is approximately x. For values up to 88, `exp(-x)` doesn't underflow (exp(-88) is tiny but non-zero), so my formula is slightly different from PyTorch's thresholded version. For example, at x=50: - PyTorch softplus: returns 50 (because x > threshold). - My formula: 50 + log1p(exp(-50)). exp(-50) ≈ 1.9e-22, which is representable in float32? Float32 min normal is ~1.17e-38, so yes, exp(-50) is representable. log1p(1.9e-22) ≈ 1.9e-22. So my sp = 50 + 1.9e-22 ≈ 50. - The difference is 1.9e-22, which is way below fp32 precision for a value of 50 (ulp is ~2^-23 * 50 ≈ 6e-6). So both represent as exactly 50 in float32. The final mish result would be identical. At x=88: - PyTorch: returns 88. - My formula: exp(-88) ≈ 6e-39, which underflows to 0 in float32 (min normal is 1.17e-38, but denormals go down to ~1.4e-45). Actually exp(-88) is about 6e-39, which is a denormal float32 value. Denormals might be flushed to zero depending on settings. If flushed to zero: my sp = 88 + 0 = 88. Same as PyTorch. At x=-88: - My formula: 0 + log1p(exp(-88)) = 0 + log1p(denormal) ≈ denormal ≈ 0. But PyTorch returns log1p(exp(-88)) which is also ≈ 0. So my mish implementation should match PyTorch to within floating point precision. The earlier correctness check showed max_abs_err=8.34e-06, confirming they match. Actually, `ConvTranspose2d(stride=2)` on input X with weight W can be computed as: For each output position (h, w), it's a dot product of the kernel with a small window of the input. As I analyzed, each output pixel depends on 1 to 4 input pixels. This can be written as a series of independent convolutions for each phase. Specifically, the output positions can be divided into 4 phases based on (h%2, w%2): - Phase (0,0): output at even rows, even cols. Each depends on exactly 1 input pixel. - Phase (0,1): output at even rows, odd cols. Each depends on exactly 2 input pixels. - Phase (1,0): output at odd rows, even cols. Each depends on exactly 2 input pixels. - Phase (1,1): output at odd rows, odd cols. Each depends on exactly 4 input pixels. Wait, is that right? Let me recheck. For output (h, w): Contributing input positions: (ih, iw) such that h = 2*ih + kh - 1 and w = 2*iw + kw - 1 for kh, kw ∈ {0,1,2}, with some boundary constraints. Actually, for interior pixels (ignoring boundaries): h even = 2k: kh must be 1. Then ih = k. h odd = 2k+1: kh can be 0 or 2. Then ih = k+1 (for kh=0) or ih = k (for kh=2). Similarly for w. So: - (even, even): kh=1, kw=1 → 1 input pixel. - (even, odd): kh=1, kw∈{0,2} → 2 input pixels. - (odd, even): kh∈{0,2}, kw=1 → 2 input pixels. - (odd, odd): kh∈{0,2}, kw∈{0,2} → 4 input pixels. Yes! A 2x2 conv-like operation for phase (1,1). More precisely, we can view each output pixel as a dot product of a small patch of the input with specific kernel weights. In many deep learning frameworks, the transposed convolution with a given weight W is equivalent to convolving the zero-inserted input with a weight that is just the transpose of W in the channel dimension, but the SAME spatially (not flipped), followed by cropping or padding. Let me verify with a concrete small example in my head. 1D case: Input x = [x0]. ConvTranspose1d with weight W = [w0, w1, w2], stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1. Output size = (1-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 1 = 2. Output: y = [y0, y1]. How is y computed in PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d? The operation is: for each input element x[i], we place the kernel W at position i*stride - padding in the output, accumulating. For i=0: place [w0, w1, w2] at position 0 - 1 = -1 in output. Position -1 wraps to... actually for transposed conv, it's more like cross-correlation with the flipped kernel? Let's think. PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d documentation says it's the transpose of Conv1d. It computes the gradient of Conv1d w.r.t. its input. For Conv1d with input length L, kernel K=3, stride=2, padding=1: Output length = floor((L + 2*1 - 3)/2) + 1 = floor((L - 1)/2) + 1. If L=2, output = floor(1/2)+1 = 1. The gradient w.r.t. input of Conv1d is ConvTranspose1d applied to grad_output with same weight. grad_input[i] = sum_j grad_output[j] * W[k] where k is determined by the conv formula. For transposed conv output at position p: y[p] = sum_{valid i, k} x[i] * W[k] where p = i*stride + k - padding (modulo some output_padding) Actually, looking at PyTorch's formula for ConvTranspose output size: out_size = (in_size - 1) * stride - 2*padding + kernel_size + output_padding. For in_size=1, stride=2, padding=1, kernel_size=3, output_padding=1: out_size = 0*2 - 2 + 3 + 1 = 2. For output position p=0: Which input positions i and kernel positions k contribute? We need p = i*stride + k - padding, i.e., 0 = 2i + k - 1. With i=0: k=1. So y[0] = x[0] * W[1]. For p=1: 1 = 2i + k - 1 → 2i + k = 2. With i=0: k=2. y[1] = x[0] * W[2]. With i=1: k=0. But i=1 is out of bounds (in_size=1). So only i=0, k=2. y[1] = x[0] * W[2]. Now, zero-interpolation + conv1d: Upsampled x_up: length 2 (since output_padding=1, we want length 2). Wait, if in_size=1, upsample by stride=2 gives length 2 (if we add trailing zeros). x_up = [x0, 0]. So the spatial flip is required: w'[j] = W[2 - j]. Wait, what happened to w'[2]? It multiplies with 0 in both cases. So we can't determine it from this tiny example. But from the boundary behavior, we expect symmetry. Actually, with in_size=2: x = [x0, x1]. ConvTranspose output size = (2-1)*2 - 2 + 3 + 1 = 4. y = [y0, y1, y2, y3]. For p=0: 0 = 2i + k - 1. i=0, k=1. y0 = x0*W[1]. For p=1: 1 = 2i + k - 1. i=0, k=2 or i=1, k=0. y1 = x0*W[2] + x1*W[0]. For p=2: 2 = 2i + k - 1. i=1, k=1. y2 = x1*W[1]. For these to match: w'[1] = W[1] w'[0] = W[2] w'[2] = W[0] Yes! w'[j] = W[2 - j]. The kernel needs to be reversed. For 2D: W'[i, j] = W[2-i, 2-j]. And for channels: W'[out, in, i, j] = W[in, out, 2-i, 2-j]. Or: W' = W.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(2, 3). So my earlier derivation was correct. Now, the question is whether `conv2d` after zero-upsampling is faster than `conv_transpose2d`. It depends on: 1. The cost of zero-upsampling (scattered write). 2. The cost of `conv2d` on 256x256 vs `conv_transpose2d` on 128x128. 3. The overhead of weight permutation and flip (one-time cost in __init__). The weight permutation is in __init__, so negligible. Zero-upsampling via `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x` on a 256x256 tensor (512MB for batch=128, C=64) is a memory-bound operation with uncoalesced writes. It might take ~1ms or more. Actually, a custom CUDA kernel for zero-upsampling could use coalesced writes! Instead of writing `x[b, c, h, w]` to `x_up[b, c, 2h, 2w]`, we could invert the loop: each thread reads one contiguous block of `x` and writes to scattered locations in `x_up`. But this still has scattered writes. Alternatively, each thread could process a block of `x_up` and read the needed `x` values. For example, a thread computing `x_up[b, c, h, w]` checks if h and w are even; if so, reads `x[b, c, h/2, w/2]`, else writes 0. This gives coalesced writes to `x_up` but scattered reads from `x`. Since `x` is much smaller than `x_up` (1/4 the size), scattered reads might be acceptable, and coalesced writes are definitely better. But then we're reading 4 times as much memory (writing 512MB, reading 128MB). Wait, if each thread writes one float to x_up, and adjacent threads write to adjacent memory in x_up, that's perfectly coalesced. The reads from x are scattered (every other row/column), but x is much smaller. Using L1/L2 cache, these reads might still be okay. So a custom zero-upsampling kernel would be much better than the PyTorch indexing approach. But this adds another kernel launch and some code complexity. Alternatively, instead of zero-upsampling explicitly, I can fold it into the conv2d custom kernel. That is, in the custom kernel, when the thread needs to read `x_up[ih, iw]`, it checks if ih and iw are both even; if so, it reads `x[ih/2, iw/2]`, otherwise it uses 0. This way, we never materialize `x_up` at all! This is actually a very nice optimization. The custom kernel for "upconv" would: 1. Compute output pixel (b, c_out, h, w). 2. Determine which input pixels contribute (up to 4). 3. For each contributing input pixel, if it corresponds to an even position in the "virtual" upsampled input, read from the actual input tensor at the compressed coordinates; otherwise, use 0. 4. But wait - in a regular conv on an upsampled input, the kernel slides over all positions. The input to the regular conv has elements at every position. For a stride=2 transposed conv, the "virtual" upsampled input has non-zeros only at even positions. So in the custom kernel, we only need to read actual input values for those even positions. For the custom kernel computing conv2d on the virtual upsampled input: Each output pixel requires reading a 3x3 patch of the virtual input (with padding). But only elements where both coordinates are even are non-zero. In a 3x3 patch centered at some location, how many even-even positions are there? For any 3x3 patch: If top-left is (even, even): positions (0,0), (0,2), (2,0), (2,2) are even-even. That's 4 positions. If top-left is (even, odd): positions (0,1), (2,1) are even-even? No, (even, odd) -> +2 -> (even, odd). None? Wait: - (even, odd): row is even, col is odd. After adding 2 to row or col: (even+2, odd) = (even, odd), (even, odd+2) = (even, odd). But we need both even. In a 3x3 patch starting at (even, odd): (0,0)=(even,odd), (0,1)=(even,even), (0,2)=(even,odd) (1,0)=(odd,odd), (1,1)=(odd,even), (1,2)=(odd,odd) (2,0)=(even,odd), (2,1)=(even,even), (2,2)=(even,odd) So even-even positions: (0,1) and (2,1). Let me think about using a really simple custom kernel where each thread computes one output pixel for all output channels. No shared memory, just direct global memory access. ```cuda __global__ void conv_transpose_mish_hardtanh_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out, float add_value, float scale) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_outputs = B * C_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total_outputs) return; // Decode idx to (b, c_out, h, w) int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int c_out = idx % C_out; idx /= C_out; int b = idx; float acc = 0.0f; // Iterate over all possible kh, kw for (int kh = 0; kh < 3; kh++) { for (int kw = 0; kw < 3; kw++) { int ih = (h + 1 - kh) / 2; int iw = (w + 1 - kw) / 2; if ((h + 1 - kh) % 2 != 0 || (w + 1 - kw) % 2 != 0) continue; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in || iw < 0 || iw >= W_in) continue; // Inner loop over c_in for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { float inp = input[((b * C_in + c_in) * H_in + ih) * W_in + iw]; float wgt = weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]; acc += inp * wgt; } } } // mish + add + hardtanh + scale float sp = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(acc))); float mish_val = acc * tanhf(sp); mish_val += add_value; mish_val = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, mish_val)); output[((b * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = mish_val * scale; } ``` Wait, this kernel uses `weight[((c_in * C_out + c_out) * 3 + kh) * 3 + kw]`. The layout is row-major. So weight[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] is at index: `((c_in * out_channels + c_out) * kernel_h + kh) * kernel_w + kw`. When virtual_h or virtual_w is odd, we skip because the virtual upsampled input would have zero there. This kernel is actually quite simple! And because it computes one output pixel per thread for one output channel, the indexing is straightforward. However, there are two issues: 1. This means each thread does 64 * 2.25 = 144 iterations on average, each with 2 global memory reads (input + weight). Total global reads per thread: ~288. Actually, for a block of 1024 threads all computing the same c_out but different (b, h, w): - Each thread reads weight[c_out, :, kh, kw] for the valid (kh, kw) positions. That's 64 * 2.25 = 144 floats. - Since all threads read the same 576 floats (the full c_out slice), and a warp reads the same addresses, these reads are broadcast. The first warp loads the weights into L1, and subsequent warps hit L1. - So total weight reads from global memory per block = 576 floats * 4 bytes = 2.25 KB. For ~524K blocks (if each block handles 1024 pixels), total weight traffic = ~1.2 GB. Very small! For input: a block of 1024 threads computing a 32x32 tile of output for a given (b, c_out) needs an input region of about 18x18 (with some variation due to the parity-based skipping). That's 324 spatial positions * 64 channels * 4 bytes = ~83KB. For adjacent tiles, there's overlap. So total input traffic is roughly B * C_in * H_in * W_in * some factor. Actually, each input pixel is read by multiple output pixels. On average, each input pixel contributes to 2.25 * C_out output pixels. So total input reads = B * C_in * H_in * W_in * 2.25 * 4 bytes = 128 * 64 * 128 * 128 * 2.25 * 4 = 1.2 GB. Total memory traffic = 1.2 GB (input) + 1.2 GB (weights) + 2.1 GB (output) = 4.5 GB. At 3.35 TB/s, this is about 1.3ms. Plus some overhead for uncoalesced access patterns due to the parity checks. So the memory bandwidth bound is at least ~1.3ms. Compare to cuDNN's ~6ms. There could be a significant speedup! But there are complications: 1. The kernel does small amounts of compute per thread, so instruction overhead might be significant. 2. The branch divergence due to parity checks (h odd/even, w odd/even) might hurt performance. About 25% of threads take the 4-input path, 25% take 1-input, 50% take 2-input. This means warps will have threads taking different paths, causing some divergence. But the divergence is not nested deeply. 3. Global memory reads for input might not be coalesced perfectly. For a warp computing adjacent w values, ih and iw depend on w parity. For example, for fixed h and a range of w: - If h is even: only kh=1 contributes. For w even: kw=1, iw = w/2. For w odd: kw∈{0,2}, iw = (w±1)/2. So adjacent threads in a warp might access input at very different iw positions, causing uncoalesced loads. This uncoalescing is a serious problem. It means the memory bandwidth utilization could be much lower than theoretical. To fix coalescing, we could organize threads so that each warp handles output pixels with the same parity. For example, process (even h, even w), (even h, odd w), (odd h, even w), (odd h, odd w) in separate kernel launches or separate thread blocks. This way, all threads in a warp follow the same path and access memory more uniformly. Alternatively, we could have each warp process 32 output pixels that are all (even, even), then the next warp processes (even, odd), etc. But in CUDA, adjacent threadIdx values execute together. We can map threadIdx to specific parities. For example, each block has phase = blockIdx.x % 4. Each block processes only one parity class. Phase 0: (h even, w even). Each thread reads 1 input value. Phase 1: (h even, w odd). Each thread reads 2 input values. Phase 2: (h odd, w even). Each thread reads 2 input values. Phase 3: (h odd, w odd). Each thread reads 4 input values. Within each phase, the memory access pattern is more uniform. But launching 4x more blocks is fine. Actually, I think the simplest effective approach is to write 4 separate kernels, one for each phase, and launch them with appropriate grid dimensions. Or combine them into one kernel with a phase parameter. But `load_inline` only supports one kernel function per wrapper... Actually no, I can define multiple global kernels in the CUDA source and call them from different wrapper functions. Or use one wrapper that launches one of 4 kernels based on a parameter. But since all 4 phases use the same output size and just different grid mappings, I could have a single kernel that processes all phases but uses a strided indexing pattern. Use `F.conv2d` on upsampled input with flipped weight. 2. Materialize the upsampled input using a custom kernel. The custom upsample kernel is very simple: ```cuda __global__ void upsample_zeros_kernel(const float* input, float* output, int B, int C, int H, int W) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C * H * W * 4; // H*2 * W*2 if (idx >= total) return; // We want to write output with mostly zeros, and non-zeros at even positions // But adjacent threads should write to adjacent memory for coalescing // We can map idx to (b, c, h, w) in output space int w = idx % (W*2); idx /= (W*2); int h = idx % (H*2); idx /= (H*2); int c = idx % C; idx /= C; int b = idx; if (h % 2 == 0 && w % 2 == 0) { int ih = h / 2; int iw = w / 2; output[idx] = input[((b * C + c) * H + ih) * W + iw]; } else { output[idx] = 0.0f; } } ``` Wait, adjacent threads have adjacent `w` values. For w=0,1,2,3,...: - w=0: even, reads input. - w=1: odd, writes 0. - w=2: even, reads input. - w=3: odd, writes 0. The reads from input are at iw = w/2 = 0, 1, 1, 2, ... for consecutive w values. So iw values for the warp are 0, 0 (skip), 1, 1 (skip), 1, 1 (skip), 2, 2 (skip)... Actually for w=0,2,4,6: iw=0,1,2,3. But threads in a warp with w=0,1,2,3... read input at iw=0, (skip), 1, (skip), ... So half the threads don't read. The threads that do read have iw values 0,1,2,3 for consecutive even w. These reads are coalesced because adjacent threads access adjacent input memory. Writes to output are always contiguous (each thread writes one float). So this kernel has coalesced writes and mostly coalesced reads. It should be very fast. After upsampling, `F.conv2d` on 256x256 with padding=1, stride=1. Total operations: 1. Upsample kernel: ~512MB write + 128MB read = 640MB at 3.35TB/s = 0.19ms. 2. conv2d: 77 GFLOPs. Even at 100 TFLOPS = 0.77ms. Total ~1ms. 3. So my elementwise kernel could be: ```cuda acc += bias[c_out]; float sp = fmaxf(acc, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(acc))); float mish_val = acc * tanhf(sp); mish_val += add_value; mish_val = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, mish_val)); output = mish_val * scale; ``` This is slightly more work per element but negligible. Alternatively, I can include bias in the conv2d call: `F.conv2d(..., bias=self.conv_transpose.bias)`. Wait, but the original reference uses `nn.ConvTranspose2d` with default bias=True, then applies mish to `conv_output + bias`. If I use `F.conv2d` with bias, it's mathematically the same: output = conv(x_up, W_c) + bias. Then I apply mish, add, hardtanh, scale. Yes! So I can simply use: ```python out = F.conv2d(x_up, self.conv_weight, bias=self.conv_transpose.bias, padding=1) ``` And then apply the mish+add+hardtanh+scale fused kernel. This is equivalent. But does using `F.conv2d` with bias perform the bias addition inside cuDNN (fused with conv)? Yes, PyTorch/cuDNN supports conv+bias fusion. Wait, I don't even need to explicitly pass bias - I can just create a `nn.Conv2d` layer with the flipped weight and the original bias, and use it normally: ```python self.conv2d = nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=1, padding=1, bias=True) self.conv2d.weight = nn.Parameter(self.conv_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(2, 3)) self.conv2d.bias = self.conv_transpose.bias ``` Then in forward: ```python B, C, H, W = x.shape x_up = torch.zeros(B, C, H*2, W*2, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x out = self.conv2d(x_up) # fused elementwise ops ``` This completely avoids writing any custom CUDA for the conv part! The only custom CUDA needed is for the elementwise ops (or I can even use PyTorch's composed ops). But the upsample step `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x` might be slow. Wait, but I said earlier that this indexing has uncoalesced writes. Is it really that slow? Let's analyze it again. `x_up` is a contiguous tensor of size [128, 64, 256, 256]. We assign `x[:, :, :, :]` to `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2]`. This is a scatter operation. PyTorch implements it as a `copy_` with a strided destination. Each thread copies one float from x to x_up. For adjacent threads (in the innermost dimension), they copy to x_up at stride 2 in the W dimension. So thread i writes to x_up[..., w*2], thread i+1 writes to x_up[..., (w+1)*2]. These are adjacent in memory! Actually, for the innermost (W) dimension, adjacent elements in x are written to positions 0, 2, 4, 6... in x_up. These are not adjacent in memory - they are separated by one float (4 bytes). So threads in a warp writing to positions 0, 2, 4, ..., 62 in the W dimension. These fall into 32 different 32-byte cache lines (or L2 sectors), but each sector contains 8 floats. So positions 0-7 are in sector 0, 8-15 in sector 1, etc. For positions 0, 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, 12, 14: these are all in sectors 0 and 1. Specifically, 0,2,4,6 are in sector 0 and 8,10,12,14 are in sector 1. A warp writes to 16 different sectors for 32 writes. Wait, for w dimension: positions 0,2,4,...,62 span 32 sectors (each sector = 32 bytes = 8 floats). Actually: - Sector 0: bytes 0-31 (floats 0-7) → w=0,2,4,6 - Sector 1: bytes 32-63 (floats 8-15) → w=8,10,12,14 - ... - Sector 7: bytes 224-255 (floats 56-63) → w=56,58,60,62 So a warp touches 8 sectors for 32 writes. That's 4x overhead compared to coalesced (1 sector per 8 writes). But it's not terrible. Similarly, in the H dimension, rows are spaced by 256*4 = 1024 bytes. Writing to row 0 and row 2 means they are 2*256*4 = 2048 bytes apart, which might be in different cache lines. Overall, the strided write is not great but not catastrophically bad. It might still be acceptable. But we can do better with a custom kernel for zero-upsampling. Since I already have the infrastructure for custom CUDA kernels, it's straightforward to write one. Actually, let me consider yet another approach: instead of zero-upsampling, I can use PyTorch's `torch.nn.functional.max_unpool2d` after a `max_pool2d` with kernel_size=1? No, that requires running max_pool2d first. How about: ```python x_up = torch.zeros(B, C, H*2, W*2, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x ``` A custom kernel for this would be very simple and much faster than the PyTorch strided copy. In the ConvTranspose2d, output_padding adds extra zeros or adjusts the output size. My zero-upsampling to exactly H*2 and W*2 gives the correct output size. But does it match the exact numerical values? For a transposed conv, output_padding affects the output size by effectively padding the output after the operation. With output_padding=1, it adds 1 to the output size compared to the base formula. For our parameters: Base output size (without output_padding) = (H-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 = 2H - 2 + 1 = 2H - 1? If we upsample x to H*2 = 16 and apply conv with padding=1: Input: 16 elements. Conv with kernel=3, padding=1, stride=1: output = 16 + 2 - 3 + 1 = 16. Correct. But does the `conv2d` with padding=1 on the upsampled input compute the same operation as the transposed conv with output_padding=1? I derived earlier that: ConvTranspose2d(x, W_t, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1) = Conv2d(UpsampleZeros(x), W_c, padding=1) where W_c = flip(W_t.permute(1,0,...)). But let me be more careful about the `output_padding`. Transposed conv with output_padding effectively does: 1. Compute the transposed conv with stride=2, padding=1 (without output_padding). 2. Pad the output with `output_padding` zeros on one side. Wait, no. `output_padding` is not padding with zeros; it's a parameter that adjusts the output size. In transposed convolution, different (stride, padding, kernel) combinations can produce the same output size. output_padding is used to resolve this ambiguity when you want a specific output size. In PyTorch, the output size formula is: `output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding` For H=8: (8-1)*2 - 2 + 3 + 1 = 14 + 2 = 16. Now, if we think of transposed conv as conv2d on upsampled input: The upsampled input without output_padding consideration would be size: `H_up = (H-1) * stride + 1 = 7*2 + 1 = 15` if we only insert stride-1 zeros between elements. Then conv2d with padding=1 gives output size = 15 + 2 - 3 + 1 = 15. To get output size 16, we need the upsampled input to be size 16. That means we need one extra zero at the end (output_padding=1 corresponds to an extra zero in the upsampled input). With `x_up = zeros(B, C, H*2, W*2)` and `x_up[:, :, ::2, ::2] = x`: For H=8, H*2 = 16. Elements are placed at indices 0, 2, 4, 6, 8, 10, 12, 14. Index 15 is zero. So yes, there's a trailing zero. This makes the effective upsampled size 16, matching output_padding=1. So the formula holds! Great. It's very simple: ```cuda __global__ void upsample_zeros_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W) { int64_t n = (int64_t)B * C * H * W; int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; for (int64_t i = idx; i < n; i += stride) { int iw = i % W; int64_t tmp = i / W; int ih = tmp % H; tmp /= H; int c = tmp % C; tmp /= C; int b = tmp; int64_t out_idx = ((int64_t)b * C + c) * (H * 2) * (W * 2) + (int64_t)ih * 2 * (W * 2) + (int64_t)iw * 2; output[out_idx] = input[i]; } } ``` Wait, this only writes the non-zero elements. Here's my plan for the final code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor upsample_zeros(torch::Tensor input, int H_out, int W_out); void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(torch::Tensor tensor, float add_val, float scale_val); ''' cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __global__ void upsample_zeros_kernel(const float* __restrict__ input, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H_in, int W_in) { int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t n = (int64_t)B * C * H_in * W_in; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; int W_out = W_in * 2; int H_out = H_in * 2; for (int64_t i = idx; i < n; i += stride) { int iw = i % W_in; int64_t tmp = i / W_in; int ih = tmp % H_in; tmp /= H_in; int c = tmp % C; int b = tmp / C; int64_t out_idx = (((int64_t)b * C + c) * H_out + (ih * 2)) * W_out + (iw * 2); output[out_idx] = input[i]; } } torch::Tensor upsample_zeros(torch::Tensor input, int H_out, int W_out) { TORCH_CHECK(input.is_cuda(), "input must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(input.is_contiguous(), "input must be contiguous"); TORCH_CHECK(input.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32, "input must be float32"); auto output = torch::zeros({input.size(0), input.size(1), H_out, W_out}, torch::dtype(torch::kFloat32).device(input.device())); int64_t n = input.numel(); if (n == 0) return output; const int threads = 512; int blocks = static_cast<int>((n + threads - 1) / threads); blocks = std::min(blocks, 8192); upsample_zeros_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), input.size(0), input.size(1), input.size(2), input.size(3)); return output; } __global__ void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel(float* __restrict__ data, float add_val, float scale_val, int64_t n) { int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; for (int64_t i = idx; i < n; i += stride) { float x = data[i]; float sp = fmaxf(x, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(x))); float th = tanhf(sp); float v = x * th; v = v + add_val; v = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, v)); data[i] = v * scale_val; } } void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(torch::Tensor tensor, float add_val, float scale_val) { TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_cuda(), "Tensor must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_contiguous(), "Tensor must be contiguous"); TORCH_CHECK(tensor.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32, "Tensor must be float32"); int64_t n = tensor.numel(); if (n == 0) return; const int threads = 512; int blocks = static_cast<int>((n + threads - 1) / threads); blocks = std::min(blocks, 8192); fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( tensor.data_ptr<float>(), add_val, scale_val, n); } ''' fused_ext = load_inline( name="fused_ops", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["upsample_zeros", "fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, add_value, scale): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding) self.add_value = add_value self.scale = scale # Prepare equivalent conv2d weight with torch.no_grad(): self.conv_weight = nn.Parameter(self.conv_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(dims=[2, 3])) if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: self.conv_bias = nn.Parameter(self.conv_transpose.bias.clone()) else: self.conv_bias = None def forward(self, x): B, C, H, W = x.shape H_out = H * self.conv_transpose.stride[0] W_out = W * self.conv_transpose.stride[1] # Upsample with zeros x_up = fused_ext.upsample_zeros(x, H_out, W_out) # Apply standard conv2d with flipped weight out = torch.nn.functional.conv2d(x_up, self.conv_weight, bias=self.conv_bias, padding=1, stride=1) # Fused elementwise fused_ext.fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(out, self.add_value, self.scale) return out ``` Wait, I need to verify this is correct! Let me think about the upsample kernel output shape. For input 128x128, H_out=256, W_out=256. The kernel writes to positions (ih*2, iw*2). The output is initialized to zeros. Yes, that matches. Also, for the bias: `self.conv_transpose.bias` has shape `[out_channels]`. `self.conv_bias` also has `[out_channels]`, and `F.conv2d` with `bias=self.conv_bias` adds it properly. Now, is the mathematical equivalence exact? Let me trace it once more for the 2D case. For original ConvTranspose2d with W_t, bias b: out_t[b, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} sum_{kh,kw} x[b, c_in, ih, iw] * W_t[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] + b[c_out] where h = 2*ih + kh - 1, w = 2*iw + kw - 1, with valid (ih, iw) and (kh, kw) in [0,2]. Wait, actually the transposed conv formula is more like scattering. For each ih, iw, kh, kw: h = 2*ih + kh - 1 w = 2*iw + kw - 1 and we add x[ih, iw] * W_t[kh, kw] + ... to output[h, w]. In our approach: x_up has non-zero at (2*ih, 2*iw). conv2d with W_c (where W_c[a,b,i,j] = W_t[b,a,2-i,2-j]) and padding=1: For output (h, w), the conv reads x_up[h-1+i, w-1+j] * W_c[c_out, c_in, i, j] for i,j in [0,2]. x_up[h-1+i, w-1+j] is non-zero only if h-1+i and w-1+j are both even. If so, it's x[(h-1+i)/2, (w-1+j)/2]. So the term is: x[ih, iw] * W_c[c_out, c_in, i, j] where ih = (h-1+i)/2, iw = (w-1+j)/2. For this to match the transposed conv term x[ih, iw] * W_t[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] at output (h, w): We need W_c[c_out, c_in, i, j] = W_t[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] where h = 2*ih + kh - 1 and w = 2*iw + kw - 1. But h = 2*ih + i - 1? Wait, from ih = (h-1+i)/2, we get h = 2*ih - i + 1. So kh = 1 - i + 1 = 2 - i? No, that's h = 2*ih + kh - 1. So 2*ih + kh - 1 = 2*ih - i + 1. kh = 2 - i. Similarly, kw = 2 - j. So W_c[c_out, c_in, i, j] = W_t[c_in, c_out, 2-i, 2-j]. Since i ranges 0..2, let i' = 2-i. Then W_c[c_out, c_in, 2-i', 2-j'] = W_t[c_in, c_out, i', j']. Or W_c[c_out, c_in, i, j] = W_t[c_in, c_out, 2-i, 2-j]. This confirms the relationship. And notice that `W_c` is indexed by `[c_out, c_in, i, j]` which is the standard conv2d layout. In my code: `self.conv_weight = self.conv_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(2, 3)` This gives: - permute(1,0,2,3): [c_out, c_in, kh, kw] - flip(2,3): [c_out, c_in, 2-kh, 2-kw] So `conv_weight[c_out, c_in, i, j] = W_t[c_in, c_out, 2-i, 2-j]`. This exactly matches `W_c`! Great, the math is solid. Now I need to make sure the CUDA code compiles and runs correctly. One issue: in the `upsample_zeros_kernel`, I compute `out_idx` with `int64_t`. Let me write the complete file: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = ''' void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(torch::Tensor tensor, float add_val, float scale_val); ''' cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> __global__ void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel(float* __restrict__ data, float add_val, float scale_val, int64_t n) { int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; for (int64_t i = idx; i < n; i += stride) { float x = data[i]; float sp = fmaxf(x, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(x))); float th = tanhf(sp); float v = x * th; v = v + add_val; v = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, v)); data[i] = v * scale_val; } } void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(torch::Tensor tensor, float add_val, float scale_val) { TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_cuda(), "Tensor must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_contiguous(), "Tensor must be contiguous"); TORCH_CHECK(tensor.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32, "Tensor must be float32"); int64_t n = tensor.numel(); if (n == 0) return; const int threads = 512; int blocks = static_cast<int>((n + threads - 1) / threads); if (blocks > 8192) blocks = 8192; fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( tensor.data_ptr<float>(), add_val, scale_val, n); } ''' fused_ext = load_inline( name="fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, add_value, scale): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding) self.add_value = add_value self.scale = scale def forward(self, x): out = self.conv_transpose(x) fused_ext.fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(out, self.add_value, self.scale) return out ``` This is clean, safe, and correct. 1) / threads);\n if (blocks > 8192) blocks = 8192;\n upsample_zeros_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C, H_in, W_in, stride);\n return output;\n}\n\n__global__ void fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel(float* __restrict__ data,\n float add_val,\n float scale_val,\n int64_t n) {\n int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x;\n for (int64_t i = idx; i < n; i += stride) {\n float x = data[i];\n float sp = fmaxf(x, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(x)));\n float th = tanhf(sp);\n float v = x * th;\n v = v + add_val;\n v = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, v));\n data[i] = v * scale_val;\n }\n}\n\nvoid fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(torch::Tensor tensor, float add_val, float scale_val) {\n TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_cuda(), \"Tensor must be CUDA\");\n TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_contiguous(), \"Tensor must be contiguous\");\n TORCH_CHECK(tensor.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32, \"Tensor must be float32\");\n int64_t n = tensor.numel();\n if (n == 0) return;\n const int threads = 512;\n int blocks = static_cast<int>((n + threads - 1) / threads);\n if (blocks > 8192) blocks = 8192;\n fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n tensor.data_ptr<float>(), add_val, scale_val, n);\n}\n'''\n\nfused_ext = load_inline(\n name=\"fused_ops\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"upsample_zeros\", \"fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, add_value, scale):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.add_value = add_value\n self.scale = scale\n self.kernel_size = kernel_size\n self.stride = stride\n self.padding = padding\n self.output_padding = output_padding\n \n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding)\n \n # Fast path only for the specific case: k=3, s=2, p=1, op=1\n self.use_fast_path = (kernel_size == 3 and stride == 2 and padding == 1 and output_padding == 1)\n if self.use_fast_path:\n with torch.no_grad():\n # ConvTranspose2d weight: [in, out, k, k]\n # Conv2d needs: [out, in, k, k] with spatial flip\n self.register_buffer('conv_weight',\n self.conv_transpose.weight.permute(1, 0, 2, 3).flip(dims=[2, 3]).contiguous())\n if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None:\n self.register_buffer('conv_bias', self.conv_transpose.bias.clone())\n else:\n self.conv_bias = None\n\n def forward(self, x):\n if self.use_fast_path:\n x_up = fused_ext.upsample_zeros(x, self.stride)\n out = torch.nn.functional.conv2d(x_up, self.conv_weight, bias=self.conv_bias, padding=1, stride=1)\n else:\n out = self.conv_transpose(x)\n fused_ext.fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(out, self.add_value, self.scale)\n return out\n" }

If reference elementwise takes ~7.7ms, total is X + 7.7ms. Then speedup = (X + 7.7) / (X + 1.3). If X = 6ms, that's 13.7 / 7.3 = 1.87x. Good! So the fused approach should provide ~1.5-2x speedup. Let me optimize the elementwise kernel with float4 and proper grid sizing to ensure it's ~1.3ms or less. Wait, but in my first profile, the total was listed as 7.6ms. If X=6ms and my elementwise kernel should take ~1.3ms, total should be ~7.3ms. So 7.6ms is very plausible for my model. For the reference, X is the same ~6ms, but elementwise ops might take ~6-8ms, totaling ~12-14ms. Speedup of ~1.7x. Actually, I realize I should check the actual reference timing. But I can't directly time it with my tools. I can only submit. Let me write a highly optimized elementwise kernel: 1. Use `float4` vectorized access. 2. Process 4 elements per thread per iteration. 3. Grid-stride loop. 4. Grid size capped at 8192 or maybe `(n + threads * vec - 1) / (threads * vec)` but capped to 128*num_sm. Actually, for H100 with 132 SMs, a good grid size for elementwise kernels is `128 * 132 = 16896` blocks. Or `8192`. Let me think about occupancy. H100 FP32 throughput is very high. Memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. For a float4 kernel: - Each warp (32 threads) loads 512 bytes and writes 512 bytes. - At 3.35TB/s, to sustain that, we need full occupancy. - Number of warps per SM: 2048 threads / 32 = 64 warps. - Number of blocks per SM: 64 warps / 16 warps per block (512 threads) = 4 blocks per SM. - Total concurrent blocks = 4 * 132 = 528. - If we have 8192 blocks, total waves = 8192 / 528 ≈ 15.5 waves. 15.5 waves is fine. The overhead of wave scheduling is minimal. If we have 262,144 blocks (for float4), that's 496 waves. That is a LOT. The launch overhead and scheduling overhead might add up. Actually, modern GPUs handle thousands of blocks well, but 500 waves is excessive. So capping to ~8192 or ~16384 is good. Let me write the final kernel with float4 and grid cap. But wait, I also need to think about instruction throughput. `expf`, `log1pf`, `tanhf` are transcendental functions. On Ampere/Hopper, `expf` is single-precision and takes about 4-6 cycles. With `--use_fast_math`, `__expf` and `__tanhf` intrinsics might be faster (lower precision but still within our tolerance?). Actually, `--use_fast_math` replaces standard functions with faster approximations, but we used `expf` which maps to the approximate version anyway. The intrinsic `__expf` is the same. On H100, transcendental throughput is typically one per SM per cycle? Not sure. Let me just write the kernel and check correctness. Vectorized version: ```cpp __global__ void kernel(float* __restrict__ data, float add_val, float scale_val, int64_t n) { int64_t idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int64_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x; int64_t n_vec = n / 4; float4* ptr = (float4*)data; for (int64_t i = idx; i < n_vec; i += stride) { float4 v = ptr[i]; // v.x float sp0 = fmaxf(v.x, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(v.x))); v.x = v.x * tanhf(sp0); v.x = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, v.x + add_val)) * scale_val; // same for y, z, w ptr[i] = v; } // Tail scalar loop int64_t start = n_vec * 4 + idx; for (int64_t i = start; i < n; i += stride) { float x = data[i]; float sp = fmaxf(x, 0.0f) + log1pf(expf(-fabsf(x))); x = x * tanhf(sp); x = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, x + add_val)) * scale_val; data[i] = x; } } ``` Let me compile this.

i < n; i += stride) {\n float x = mishf(data[i]);\n x = fminf(1.0f, fmaxf(-1.0f, x + add_val)) * scale_val;\n data[i] = x;\n }\n}\n\nvoid fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(torch::Tensor tensor, float add_val, float scale_val) {\n TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_cuda(), \"Tensor must be CUDA\");\n TORCH_CHECK(tensor.is_contiguous(), \"Tensor must be contiguous\");\n TORCH_CHECK(tensor.scalar_type() == torch::kFloat32, \"Tensor must be float32\");\n int64_t n = tensor.numel();\n if (n == 0) return;\n const int threads = 512;\n int64_t blocks_needed = (n / 4 + threads - 1) / threads;\n if (blocks_needed < 1) blocks_needed = 1;\n int blocks = static_cast<int>(blocks_needed);\n if (blocks > 8192) blocks = 8192;\n fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>(\n tensor.data_ptr<float>(), add_val, scale_val, n);\n}\n'''\n\nfused_ext = load_inline(\n name=\"fused_mish_v3\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False,\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, add_value, scale):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding)\n self.add_value = add_value\n self.scale = scale\n\n def forward(self, x):\n out = self.conv_transpose(x)\n fused_ext.fused_mish_add_hardtanh_scale(out, self.add_value, self.scale)\n return out\n" }
